THE SINGLE FEDERAL STATUTE THAT SHUTS IT ALL DOWN: FALSE CLAIMS ACT (31 U.S.C. §§ 3729-3733): The FCA prohibits knowingly presenting (or causing to be presented) to the federal government a false or fraudulent claim for payment or approval.     1058: 30. FCA liability: Knowingly submitting a false claim makes the wrongdoer subject to treble damages (three times the amount of defrauded funds) plus civil penalties. (Source: False Claims Act, 31 U.S.C. § 3729)[reference:1].
    1059: 31. FCA qui tam provision: The FCA allows a private citizen (called a "relator") to file a lawsuit on behalf of the United States against anyone committing fraud against the federal government. If the lawsuit succeeds, the relator can receive a reward of 15-30% of the recovery. The government can also join the case. (Source: 31 U.S.C. § 3730)[reference:2].
    1060: 32. FCA as the shutdown mechanism: A single qui tam lawsuit under the FCA would: (a) Expose the false "clinic" billing, (b) Freeze federal funding pending investigation, (c) Trigger treble damages that bankrupt the operation, (d) Make every participant—city, county, Mercy House, ambulance company—liable, (e) Require an immediate halt to operations while the fraud is investigated. (Source: FCA legal framework).
    1061: 33. Why the FCA works when nothing else does: The state and county have immunity shields for negligence and civil rights claims. But the FCA bypasses all of them. It is a federal fraud statute. Sovereign immunity does not apply to fraud against the United States. (Source: FCA case law).
    1062: 34. Who can file a qui tam: Any person with original, non-public information about the fraud can file a complaint under seal with the Department of Justice. The DOJ investigates. If they find fraud, they intervene. If they decline, the relator can proceed alone. (Source: 31 U.S.C. § 3730)[reference:3].
    1063: 35. Statute of limitations: Six years from the date of the violation, or three years from the date the fraud should have been discovered. (Source: 31 U.S.C. § 3731).

    1658: 
    1659: Comprehensive, shelter-level data for the last four years is not publicly reported or standardized in a way that makes a request like this straightforward. After a thorough search, I've found a few key reasons for this:
    1660: 
    1661: *   **Data Silos**: Annual reports and detailed operational data are often hosted on individual organization websites and are not aggregated by the county into a single, accessible dataset.
    1662: *   **Year Overlap**: Many organizations operate on fiscal years that don't align with the calendar year (e.g., July–June), making direct yearly comparisons difficult.
    1663: *   **Disclosure Limits**: Shelters for survivors of domestic violence are legally required to keep their locations and resident data confidential for safety. Publicly available information is extremely limited.
    1664: *   **Category Mismatch**: The total "children" can include youth of different ages, depending on the organization's definition.
    1665: *   **Different "Served" vs. "Housed"**: An organization might report the total number of children its programs served (including non-residential services), not just those who spent the night. This can inflate the numbers compared to overnight capacity.
    1666: 
    1667: ### 📊 The Best Available Data
    1668:
